Tooltip\">Morgan Stanley’s Peter Carr, Noted Derivatives Expert, to Chair NYU Tandon Finance and Risk Enginee</a>\n\t\t\t\t</h3>\n\t\t\t\t<div class=\"ctaFtThreadTextAvatarPage a.ctaFtThreadTextAvatarPage baseHtml\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t<b>Lays Out Plans to Attract Additional Stellar Practitioners to One of the Oldest and Largest Financial Engineering Educational Programs</b><br>\n<br>\nBROOKLYN, New York – One of Morgan Stanley’s top quants is returning to his academic roots full time, as chair of one of the world’s oldest and largest financial engineering programs. The NYU Tandon School of Engineering announced it appointed Peter P. Carr – managing director and head of market modeling at Morgan Stanley since 2010 – chair of its Department of Finance and Risk Engineering.<br>\n<br>\nCarr, a recognized expert on derivatives who has produced seminal work on volatility, remained a prolific researcher and adjunct professor throughout his professional career at Morgan Stanley, Bloomberg LP, and Banc of America Securities.<br>\n<br>\nHe was honored as Quant of the Year by <i>Risk Magazine, </i>shared the ISA Medal for Science from the University of Bologna, and was named Financial Engineer of the Year by the baseHtml\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t<b>An Interesting Development of an Offshore Strategy</b><br>\n<br>\nI was talking to the head of fixed income Strategy at a major investment bank last week.  He described their current hiring pattern as follows:<br>\n<ul>\n<li>Junior quants – campus recruiting</li>\n<li>Mid-level quants – <i>imported from offshore locations</i></li>\n</ul>I thought that latter was really interesting.  What a smart move to let talent bubble up through the lower cost regions, then import that talent, a known commodity, to the head office.  The strategy provides great career growth for the individuals involved, is less expensive for the firm, and less risky than an outside hire.  This strategy has obvious implications for quants in NYC who are considering a lateral move.  To be sure, this is one observation, not necessarily an industry trend.<br>\n<br>\n<b>CDS Market is Dead</b><br>\n<br>\nI wasn’t paying attention, but the CDS market has died. Tooltip\">The Value of a PhD and MFE Degree</a>\n\t\t\t\t</h3>\n\t\t\t\t<div class=\"ctaFtThreadTextAvatarPage a.ctaFtThreadTextAvatarPage baseHtml\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t<div style=\"text-align: center\">\n\t\n\t<img src=\"https://www.quantnet.com/attachments/wall-street-jpg.8252/\" alt=\"Wall-Street.jpg\" class=\"bbCodeImage LbImage\" style=\"\">\n\t\t\n​</div>Since writing for this blog in January about the HFT/algo job market, I’ve received many inquiries from students asking about the “requirements” for quant jobs on Wall Street.  “Do I need a PhD?” is a frequent question.  Each time I receive one of these inquiries, I struggle with the answer.  My instinct is no.  But when I look at who is working in these jobs, I do see a predominance of PhD’s in the top positions.  PhD’s in mathematics, physics, operations research, EE, etc. are common in the quant community.  So it’s tempting to tell students that a PhD is helpful, but it feels like the wrong answer.  In my gut I know that the people getting these jobs are not getting offers because they have extra letters after their name.  The people in these positions are there because they have proved over their academic and professional lives that they are:<span class=\"prbreak\" style=\"display: none\"></span><br>\n<b>List 1</b><br>\n<ul>\n<li>Very smart</li>\n<li>Quantitative thinkers</li>\n<li>Good at...</li>\n</ul>\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t<div class=\"ctaFtThreadTitleLinkPage Tooltip\">Do quant algo and HF trading jobs still exist?</a>\n\t\t\t\t</h3>\n\t\t\t\t<div class=\"ctaFtThreadTextAvatarPage a.ctaFtThreadTextAvatarPage baseHtml\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\tAt the end of last year I sent a State-of-the-Talent-Market email to colleagues.  In that mail I wrote:<br>\n\n\n\n<div class=\"bbCodeBlock bbCodeQuote\">\n\t<aside>\n\t\t\n\t\t<blockquote class=\"quoteContainer\"><div class=\"quote\"><b>Candidate’s Market:</b> As always, the market for the best technical talent is extremely tight.  Unless you are offering the most desirable of jobs, you need to work to attract the talent you want.  Good candidates are currently favoring the following:<br>\n<ul>\n<li>Algo/HFT</li>\n<li>Buy-side</li>\n<li>Front-office (nothing new)</li>\n<li>Data analytics</li>\n</ul></div><div class=\"quoteExpand\">Click to expand...</div></blockquote>\n\t</aside>\n</div>@<a href=\"https://www.quantnet.com/members/2940/\" class=\"username\" data-user=\"2940, Ken Abbott\">Ken Abbott</a>, a frequent contributor to QuantNet, replied to my mail:<br>\n\n\n\n<div class=\"bbCodeBlock bbCodeQuote\">\n\t<aside>\n\t\t\n\t\t<blockquote class=\"quoteContainer\"><div class=\"quote\">Are there really jobs in algo and HF trading?  I hear all these kids in my classes talk about it, but I hear of few people getting these jobs and even fewer actually making money.</div><div class=\"quoteExpand\">Click to expand...</div></blockquote>\n\t</aside>\n</div>Subsequently, he asked if I would elaborate on the job market in algo and HFT for this blog.<br>\n<br>\nThe simple answer is yes, there <i>are</i> jobs in Algo/HFT, and some of them are paying very well. style=\"\">\n\t\t\n​</div><i>The job market for quants has changed inexorably. The “particle finance” trend of the last 20 years is on the wane. While funds will still be able to trade on a prop basis, banks’ ability to do so has been severely restricted.<br>\nSome may see this as a pendulum, but most agree that the aggressive trading styles seen in regulated financial institutions will never be seen again. <br>\n<br>\nDoes that mean that there are no more jobs for quants? Certainly not. It does mean, however, that the nature of the job market will be different. The growing number of quant finance programs also suggests that there will be much more competition for these jobs. <br>\n<br>\nThe following suggestions may be helpful in the job hunt.<span Tooltip\">How to evaluate your new job offer</a>\n\t\t\t\t</h3>\n\t\t\t\t<div class=\"ctaFtThreadTextAvatarPage a.ctaFtThreadTextAvatarPage baseHtml\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\tAfter conducting an exhaustive job-search campaign, you finally received an offer –congratulations! There are many factors to consider when examining this new offer. Evaluating a job offer is very subjective, but people often focus on the salary and disregard other key areas. Here is a five-step process that I developed to help my clients fully evaluate new job opportunities and determine if this is the right fit for them.<span class=\"prbreak\" style=\"display: none\"></span><br>\n<br>\n<ol>\n<li><b>Evaluate the Position</b>: The actual position is the most important part of the offer. In this new economy, where jobs tend to have a shorter tenure, each position becomes the stepping-stone to the next position. So if you have a career path in mind, each job positions you for the next step in your career. Is this opportunity the right one for you at this time? Would this position align you with your intended career path? Do you have the necessary skills to do this job well, and at the same time does this job provide you with...</li>\n</ol>\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t<div
